<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of cultivated peanut provides insight into legume karyotypes, polyploid evolution and crop domestication"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-05-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature America, Inc."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="High oil and protein content make tetraploid peanut a leading oil and food legume. Here we report a high-quality peanut genome sequence, comprising 2.54&#8201;Gb with 20 pseudomolecules and 83,709 protein-coding gene models. We characterize gene functional groups implicated in seed size evolution, seed oil content, disease resistance and symbiotic nitrogen fixation. The peanut B subgenome has more genes and general expression dominance, temporally associated with long-terminal-repeat expansion in the A subgenome that also raises questions about the A-genome progenitor. The polyploid genome provided insights into the evolution of Arachis hypogaea and other legume chromosomes. Resequencing of 52 accessions suggests that independent domestications formed peanut ecotypes. Whereas 0.42&#8211;0.47 million years ago (Ma) polyploidy constrained genetic variation, the peanut genome sequence aids mapping and candidate-gene discovery for traits such as seed size and color, foliar disease resistance and others, also providing a cornerstone for functional genomics and peanut improvement. High-quality genome sequence of cultivated peanut comprising 2.54&#8201;Gb with 20 pseudomolecules and 83,709 protein-coding gene models provides insights into genome evolution and the genetic mechanisms underlying seed size and leaf resistance in peanut."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>High oil and protein content make tetraploid peanut a leading oil and food legume. Here we report a high-quality peanut genome sequence, comprising 2.54âGb with 20 pseudomolecules and 83,709 protein-coding gene models. We characterize gene functional groups implicated in seed size evolution, seed oil content, disease resistance and symbiotic nitrogen fixation. The peanut B subgenome has more genes and general expression dominance, temporally associated with long-terminal-repeat expansion in the A subgenome that also raises questions about the A-genome progenitor. The polyploid genome provided insights into the evolution of <i>Arachis hypogaea</i> and other legume chromosomes. Resequencing of 52 accessions suggests that independent domestications formed peanut ecotypes. Whereas 0.42â0.47 million years ago (Ma) polyploidy constrained genetic variation, the peanut genome sequence aids mapping and candidate-gene discovery for traits such as seed size and color, foliar disease resistance and others, also providing a cornerstone for functional genomics and peanut improvement.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Cultivated peanut or groundnut (<i>A. hypogaea</i> L.) is among the most important oil and food legumes, grown on 25 million ha between latitudes 40Â°âN and 40Â°âS with annual production of ~46 million tons (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home">http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home</a>). It presumably was domesticated in South America ~6,000 years ago and then was widely distributed in post-Columbian times<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1631">1</a></sup>. Combining richness in seed oil (~46â58%) and protein (~22â32%), peanut is important in fighting malnutrition and ensuring food security. A âlongevity fruitâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chen, X. et al. Draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor (Arachis duranensis) provides insights into geocarpy, oil biosynthesis, and allergens. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 6785â6790 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1635">2</a></sup>, peanut also offers health benefits such as richness in heart-healthy oleic and linoleic acid; resveratrol, fiber and folic acid; and easily digested protein. In Asia and Africa, more peanut is produced than any other grain legume including soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1639">1</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home">http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home</a>). In China, peanut accounts for &gt;46% of total output of all oil crops, ranking fourth after rice, wheat and corn in market value. A nitrogen-fixing <i>Fabaceae</i> plant with geocarpy, peanut can grow on arid and marginal land<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tan, D., Zhang, Y. &amp; Wang, A. A review of geocarpy and amphicarpy in angiosperms, with special reference to their ecological adaptive significance. Chin. J. Plant Ecol. 34, 72â88 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1651">3</a></sup>. With yield averaging 3,649âkgâha<sup>â1</sup> in China in recent years and especially with the advent of high oleic acid cultivars, peanut is increasingly important as an oil and protein source.</p><p>The <i>Arachis</i> genus contains 81 species, mostly diploids (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â20). Genetic, cytogenetic, phylogeographic and molecular evidence suggested that hybridization between diploids <i>A. duranensis</i> (AA genome) and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (BB) may have formed the allotetraploid <i>A. hypogaea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Robledo, G., Lavia, G. I. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relations among wild Arachis species with the A genome as revealed by FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection. Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 1295â1307 (2009)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1678">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1151â1165 (2012)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1678_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Seijo, G. et al. Genomic relationships between the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea, Leguminosae) and its close relatives revealed by double GISH. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1963â1971 (2007)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1678_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ramos, M. L. et al. Chromosomal and phylogenetic context for conglutin genes in Arachis based on genomic sequence. Mol. Genet. Genetica 143, 113â125 (2015)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1678_4">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="FÃ¡vero, A. Although agronomic traits differ dramatically between cultivated peanut and its wild progenitors, cytogenetic and genetic studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Samoluk, S. P., Simpson, C. E., Valls, F. M. J. &amp; Velo, N. A. Study of evolution of cultivated peanut through crossability studies among Arachis ipaensis, A. duranensis and A. hypogaea. Crop Sci. 46, 1546â1552 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1720">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nielen, S. et al. Matita, a new retroelement from peanut: Characterization and evolutionary context in the light of the Arachis A-B genome divergence. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 21â38 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1723">14</a></sup> suggest few changes in the A and B subgenomes since polyploidization.</p><p>The complexity resulting from closely related subgenomes, repetitive sequence abundance and large genome size (2.7âGb) complicates peanut genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1730">1</a></sup>. We present a reference genome sequence of cultivated peanut to facilitate understanding of genome architecture and accelerate crop improvement. Resequencing of 30 allotetraploid accessions of various ecotypes, 18 wild species and four synthetic tetraploids provides insights into peanut genome architecture, trait biology, evolution and domestication.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>A reference genome assembly was developed for <i>A. hypogaea</i> var. Shitouqi (zh.h0235, a well-known Chinese cultivar and breeding parent belonging to subspecies <i>fastigiata</i>, botanical type <i>vulgaris</i> and agronomic type Spanish with heterozygosity only 1/6,537 nucleotides on average) (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.1</a>). First, assembly of 100Ã single-molecule real-time sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jarvis, D. E. et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature 542, 307â312 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1758">15</a></sup> yielded contigs totaling 2.54âGb, 94% of estimated peanut genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1763">1</a></sup>, with N50 (the shortest contig length at 50% of the total assmbled genome accumulated from the longest one) of 1.51âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.3</a>). Approximately 90% of the assembly was contributed by just 1,804 contigs (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.1</a>). Assembly thresholds of overlapping reads &gt;96% and overlapping length &gt;2,000 base pairs (bp) were used. Second, chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) sequencing produced 31,734,151 valid paired-end reads that covered 99.6% of assembly length. This allowed assembly of PacBio contigs into 20 chromosome-scale scaffolds with N50 of 129.8âMb, accounting for 95.5% of assembled sequences (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1</a>) after dissociating 297 mistakenly assembled contigs by three-dimensional proximity information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kalhor, R. et al. Genome architectures revealed by tethered chromosome conformation capture and population based modeling. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 90â98 (2011)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1789">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289â293 (2009)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1789_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d8005e1792">18</a></sup> and/or genetic mapping.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Peanut genome assembly statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To support chromosome assembly, we integrated two new genetic maps with two existing genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhou, X. et al. Construction of a SNP-based genetic linkage map in cultivated peanut based on large scale marker development using next-generation double-digest restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (ddRADseq). BMC Genom. 15, 351 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2082">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Peanut Marker Database (Kazusa DNA Research Institute, 2016); &#xA;http://marker.kazusa.or.jp/Peanut/&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2085">20</a></sup> through ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: Robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2089">21</a></sup>, yielding 14,619 loci in 20 linkage groups covering 3,264.4 cM (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1</a>; Supplementary Datasets <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM3">3</a>). Finally, 20 pseudomolecules were created by anchoring 6,289 contigs to the genetic and Hi-C scaffold maps through ALLMAPS together with minor manual adjustment of five Hi-C assembled error scaffolds based on genetic maps. The 2.51âGb total size of pseudomolecules is 98.75% of total assembly length (chromosomes are designated Chr01âChr20, corresponding to A01âA10 and B01âB10 of the diploid A and B chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2106">1</a></sup>, respectively). The remaining contigs (32.3âMb) were designated chromosome_00 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM3">3</a>). High co-linearity between the assemblies and published BACs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ratnaparkhe, M. B. et al. Comparative analysis of peanut NBS-LRR gene clusters suggests evolutionary innovation among duplicated domains and erosion of gene microsynteny. New Phytol. 192, 164â178 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2116">22</a></sup> and 1,576 <i>A. hypogaea</i> BAC paired-end sequences (GenBank accession numbers <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/FI498696.1">FI498696.1</a> to <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/FI503143.1">FI503143.1</a>; <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucgss?term=Bactierial+artificial+chromosome+of+Arachis+hypogaea">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucgss?term=Bactierial+artificial+chromosome+of+Arachis+hypogaea</a>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM4">4a</a>), low consensus error rate and high contig read depth (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM4">4b,c</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.8</a>) all indicated high assembly quality.</p><p>A total of 83,709 protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2a</a>) were inferred from the assembly using ab initio prediction with supporting evidence of RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) data from 39 tissues/conditions and PacBio isoform sequencing (Iso-Seq) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2c</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM5">5a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">4a,c</a>). On average, the genes encode transcripts of 1,589.5âbp with 6.8 exons and proteins of 403 amino acids, comparable to other legumes but longer than the diploid A and B genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2164">1</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2a,b</a>). Among coding gene models, 62,781 were annotated with annotation edit distance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yandell, M. &amp; Ence, D. A beginnerâs guide to eukaryotic genome annotation. Nat. Rev. Genet. 13, 329â342 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2172">23</a></sup> values â¤0.38 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">4d</a>). Approximately 76.6% of predicted genes were assigned functional annotations (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2d</a>). We identified 39,127 non-coding RNA (ncRNA) including 4,723 transfer RNAs (tRNAs), 3,107 ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs), 480 microRNAs (miRNAs) and 30,817 small nuclear RNAs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3a</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM5">5b</a>). A total of 1.97âGb (77.65%) of genome sequences was repetitive, including 1.67âGb (64.74%) of retrotransposons and 114âMb (4.49%) of DNA transposons (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5</a>). Gypsy and nonautonomous long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons comprised 40.59% and 27.14% of the genome, respectively. Identification of 93.1% of the 1,440 genes in the Plantae BUSCO dataset<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2195">24</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">4</a>) indicated high quality of genome assembly and annotation.</p><p>Peanut genome complexity combines allotetraploidy with other gene duplication mechanisms. A total of 30,596 nonredundant peanut genes including 24,208 (79.12%) with and 6,338 (20.88%) without homeologs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6</a>) were identified. Among the 6,388 genes without homeologs, 2,421 appear to have formed since tetraploidy, and some might be false annotations because 47% (1,140) of tetraploid-specific genes had an annotation edit distance larger than 0.4. We also detected 27,913 duplicated genes with 10,590 and 17,323 in subgenomes A and B (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM5">5c</a>), including 2,402 tandem (consecutive) and 25,511 dispersed duplications (on different chromosomes or apart in the same chromosome) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6</a>). In 29 RNA samples, the 24,208 homeologous pairs showed widespread differential expression (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig1">1a</a>), with dominant expression more frequent among B than A subgenome homeologs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Expression differentiation of paired homeologous genes between peanut subgenomes and repeat expansion among peanut and diploid ancestor genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Expression differentiation of paired homeologous genes between peanut subgenomes and repeat expansion among peanut and diploid ancestor genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1123" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Widespread expression differentiation of homeologous gene pairs between two subgenomes is shown. Homeologous chromosomes are indicated at the bottom of the figure. <b>b</b>, Density distribution of substitution rates using the paired-end sequences of LTR retrotransposons in the <i>A. hypogaea</i>, <i>A. hypogaea</i>-SubA, <i>A. hypogaea</i>-SubB, <i>A. duranenesis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> genomes. The LTR in <i>A. hypogaea</i> and <i>A. hypogaea</i>-SubA exhibited rapid expansion ~246,700 years ago, but those of <i>A. hypogaea</i>-SubB, <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> did so about 0.8922, 1.1206 and 1.0049 Ma, respectively, based on the formula <i>T</i>â=â<i>S</i>/2âÂµ (where <i>T</i> is the evolution time, <i>S</i> is the substitution rate here and Âµ is the 1.64âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> substitution rate per year; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.3.5</a>). The number of LTR retrotransposons and the peak substitution rate for each part are embedded in the figure.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Characterization of subgenome structure</h3><p>The B subgenome is more similar to <i>A. ipaensis</i> than the A subgenome is to <i>A. duranensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2297">1</a></sup> (also shown in Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>) with 2,543 (1,408.4âMb, 55.49% of contig length, &gt;93.28% identity) anchored B genome contigs, versus 2,477 (1,085.7âMb, 42.77%, &gt;92.82% identity) A genome contigs (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM6">6</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.3.1</a>). The diploid A genome and tetraploid A subgenome shared 34,266 co-linear genes, whereas the diploid B genome and tetraploid B subgenome shared 38,417, also indicating better preservation of gene co-linearity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig2">2a,b</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5a</a>). There was also better co-linearity of the B subgenome with other legumes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>), reflected by 2,067 gene pairs in 301 co-linear blocks with more than 4 homeologous gene pairs in the A subgenome and 2,283 gene pairs in 300 blocks in the B subgenome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>). A total of 629 genes (1.8% of 35,576) might have been affected by gene conversion, with 369 (58.7%) A subgenome genes converted by their B subgenome counterparts and 230 (41.3%) vice versa (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.2</a>). Well-preserved sequence homology after tetraploidization may facilitate inter-subgenome recombination and rearrangement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2333">25</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Characterization of the peanut genome and chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Characterization of the peanut genome and chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="730" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Circos diagram depicting relationships of A and B subgenome chromosomal pseudomolecules. The scale for the chromosomes (outer bars) is megabases; colors represent the density of nonautonomous LTR retrotransposons and Ty3-gypsy elements (blue) and genes (green). Homeologous blocks of â¥30 gene pairs between Chr01âChr10 and Chr11âChr20 (A01âA10 and B01âB10, respectively) are connected with lines. <b>b</b>, Syntenic comparisons between peanut subgenomes and diploid A and B genomes. The outer three circles are chromosomes, density of genes and of Ty3-gypsy and nonautonomous LTR retrotransposons (as shown in <b>a</b>). Colored lines connect blocks with â¥30 orthologous gene pairs between the A and B subgenomes and <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> genomes, respectively, based on BLASTN. <b>c</b>, Alignment of diploid peanut A03 and B03 contigs to corresponding tetraploid chromosomes, with parameters: -a 8 -p blastn -m 9 -e 1e-10. The best hits with alignment length â¥2,000 bp were plotted. Translocation between chromosomes A03 and B03 is evident in cultivated peanut. <b>d</b>, Eleven-genome alignment using co-linear genes, each mapped onto the barrel medic chromosomes. A, <i>A. hypogaea</i> A; B, <i>A. hypogaea</i> B; C, <i>C. cajan</i>; D, <i>A. duranensis</i>; E, <i>C. arietinum</i>; G, <i>G. max</i>; I, <i>A. ipaensis</i>; L, <i>L. japonicus</i>; M, <i>M. truncatula</i>; P, <i>P. vulgaris</i>; R, <i>V. radiata</i>; U, <i>V. angularis</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Unbalanced structural rearrangements occurred in the peanut A (sub)genome before and after divergence. Reciprocal comparisons of corresponding diploid and tetraploid chromosomes (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.2</a>) identified at least six exchanges or substitutions with clearly defined boundaries between A and B subgenomes, including a 10-Mb translocation between chromosomes 3 and 13 (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig2">2c</a>). Inversions affecting â¥3âMb in the A (sub)genome (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>) included 11 inter-subgenome incongruent chromosomal regions, 11 inter-A (sub)genome regions, and 4 inter-B (sub)genome regions. These comprise 23 independent events, 21 (91.3%) occurring in the A lineage (<i>Î§</i><sup>2</sup> test <i>P</i>â=â7.4âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>). Interestingly, B-genome-specific crossover occurred in the B (but not A) lineage to make chromosomes 7 and 8 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9e</a>), and the changes were retained in the A genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2443">1</a></sup> to A subgenome as clearly shown in chromosome 8, which demonstrated irregular gene density distribution patterns (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7b</a>).</p><p>Most transposable elements expanded after tetraploidization, especially the Gypsy and unclassified LTRs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3b</a>). B subgenome LTRs are derived from the progenitor B genome, but most A subgenome LTRs formed after polyploidization (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig1">1b</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.3.5</a>). Base substitution rates between paired-end sequences showed that the A subgenome and <i>A. hypogaea</i> experienced rapid LTR expansion after tetraploidization (~0.25 Ma), but LTRs of the B subgenome and the two diploids expanded before tetraploidization (0.89, 1.12 and 1.00 Ma), respectively. A subgenome LTR expansion may relate to the prevalence of dysfunctional expression or loss of A subgenome homeologous genes in polyploid peanut<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wendel, J. F. et al. The long and short of doubling down: Polyploidy, epigenetics, and the temporal dynamics of genome fractionation. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 49, 1â7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2465">26</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig1">1b</a>), and also raised questions about whether the sequenced <i>A. duranensis</i> was representative of the A subgenome progenitor.</p><p>Traces of legume-common tetraploidy (LCT) ~59 Ma (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2479">27</a></sup>) and core-eudicot-common hexaploidy (ECH) ~130 Ma remain in the peanut genome. Post-ECH genome structure has been well preserved in <i>V. vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2485">28</a></sup>, and post-LCT structure in <i>P. vulgaris</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.4.1</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9a,b</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>). The A subgenome conserved 1,289 co-linear gene pairs from LCT and 1,198 from ECH, accounting for 6.9% and 6.5% of gene content, and the B subgenome contained 1,508 and 1,372 from LCT and ECH (6.6% and 6.1% of its gene content), respectively. Peanut often preserves ancestral gene arrangements in comparison with other legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wendel, J. F. et al. The long and short of doubling down: Polyploidy, epigenetics, and the temporal dynamics of genome fractionation. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 49, 1â7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2502">26</a></sup> (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig2">2d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">10</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Legume karyotype evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Legume karyotype evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="420" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The 16 ancestral legume chromosomes (called Lu, denoted by capital letters AâQ), were reconstructed by using corresponding common bean genes and compared with extant legume genomes. By using dot plots between Lu and each legume genome, and between close legume relatives, we reconstruct the origin of peanut chromosomes, including <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The peanut karyotype formed largely independently from those of other legumes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.5</a>). âTop-downâ grapeâlegume comparison (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9a,b</a>; Fig. 10) identified 5 independent chromosome fusions, including 3 nested chromosome fusions and 2 endâend joins, producing 16 basic legume ancestral chromosomes before the LCT (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9c</a>). A bottom-up approach found 5 common bean chromosomes and 11 chromosomal blocks largely preserved in different legumes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9d</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>), identifying 16 post-LCT ancestral chromosomes. This means that after doubling in the LCT, the original chromosome number was restored after genome repatterning, resembling maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Schnable, J. C. et al. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069â4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2566">29</a></sup>. Comparison with the 16 post-LCT chromosomes (called Lu), reconstructed by using common bean genes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8d</a>), revealed peanut ancestral chromosomes A1, A3, A4, A5, A6 and A7 to be composed of segments originated from Lu chromosomes via six fusions resulting in chromosome number reduction. Chromosomes A2, A8, A9 and A10 were produced by two crossovers of Lu chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>). After splitting from the A genome, crossing over in the peanut B genome produced its specific chromosomes 7 and 8 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9e</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Changes of subgenome content</h3><p>The peanut A (37,059 genes) and B (46,650 genes) subgenomes, respectively, showed 0.88% and 12.46% expansion in gene content compared with <i>A. duranensis</i> (A) and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (B)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2600">1</a></sup>, supporting dominance of the B subgenome. Compared with related legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2604">27</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, the cultivated peanut genome shared 9,614 orthologous groups/families of genes (53.45% of the total identified) with soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2612">30</a></sup>, common bean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schmutz, J. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707â713 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2616">31</a></sup>, <i>Medicago</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520â524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2622">32</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM7">9</a>). Of 24,380 orthologous gene families identified in A and B diploid genomes, 22,109 (90.68%) were retained in peanut after tetraploidization (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">11</a>). Among ortholog gene sets with only one copy found in all three <i>Arachis</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2645">1</a></sup>, 1,162 and 939 genes were lost from the peanut A and B subgenomes, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4b</a>). Furthermore, 7,714 genes that are single copy in each diploid remained single copy in both peanut subgenomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Peanut gene retention after tetraploidization."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Peanut gene retention after tetraploidization.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="651" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Numbers of shared and unique orthologous protein-coding gene clusters in peanut (AHAB), <i>A. duranensis</i> (Aradu) and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (Araip). <b>b</b>, The number of single-copy gene sets is presented (blue), retained as a single copy (orange) or lost (gray) in the peanut A or B subgenomes. <b>c</b>, Maximum likelihood tree of ARF gene family, with 114, 28 and 28 members in peanut, <i>A. duranensis</i>, and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively. Branch values represent the percentage of 1,000 bootstrap replicates supporting the topology. Scale bar represents substitutions per site. <b>d</b>, Chromosome distributions of genes involved in fatty acid metabolism, symbiotic nitrogen fixation pathways and biotic stress resistance in cultivated peanut, from outer to inner circles representing chromosomes, R genes, acyl-lipid-related and nodulation-related genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 10,974 gene families had 3â51 copies in tetraploid peanut (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM5">5c</a>). For example, peanut, <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively, have 114, 28 and 28 members of âAuxin response factorâ (ARF), which regulates plant growth and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Okushima, Y. et al. Auxin response factor 2 (arf2): A pleiotropic developmental regulator. Plant J. 43, 29â46 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2701">33</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4c</a>). The ARF genes group into nine clusters, with IâV including only copies from cultivated peanut, perhaps related to large seed size and organ evolution. Peanut contains three copies of cytochrome P450 78A6 genes (CYP78A6) with two duplicated members compared with just single copies in the diploid B genome, associated with large seed size. Among 3,044 orthologous families of 10,339 genes specific to peanut (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">8</a>), many have functional gene ontology relating to nucleic acid-binding proteins, transcription factors, ATPâNADH-related or ARFs.</p><p>The total of 661 genes detected in tetraploid peanut with nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domains characteristic of biotic stress resistance were fewer than the sum of those in <i>A. duranensis</i> (385) and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (428) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2721">1</a></sup>), and mostly located in terminal regions of chromosomes, particularly Chr02 and Chr04 (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM8">10</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4d</a>). These genes comprised three groups: coiled coil (CC)-NBS-leucine-rich repeat (LRR) (CNL), Toll/interluekin-1 receptor (TIR)-NBS-LRR (TNL) and (albeit few) resistance to powdery mildew8 (RPW8)-NBS-LRR (RNL) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">12</a>). Many CNL were absent in peanut, suggesting some losses during domestication, although retained TNL numbers were comparable with wild species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">12b,c</a>).</p><p>Seed oil content and quality are primary targets for peanut breeding programs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Pandey, M. K. et al. Identification of QTLs associated with oil content and mapping FAD2 genes and their relative contribution to oil quality in peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.). BMC Genet. 15, 133 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2741">34</a></sup>. We identified a total of 1,944 acyl-lipid orthologs in peanut, with 1,347 and 1,324 in <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2750">1</a></sup>, respectively (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM9">11a</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4d</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">9</a>). These genes grouped into 727 gene families. In 50 gene families, the single-copy gene of wild peanut was duplicated at least once in cultivated peanut including six families of 23 genes with more than two duplicates, responsible for fatty acid synthesis, lipid signaling and triacylglycerol (TAG) biosynthesis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">10</a>). At least 426 genes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM9">11a</a>) were located within 125 published quantitative trait locus (QTL) regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shasidhar, Y. et al. Molecular mapping of oil content and fatty acids using dense genetic maps in groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). Front. Plant Sci. 8, 794 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2770">35</a></sup>, comprising candidates for future cloning and oil improvement. We constructed a genome-scale acyl-lipid metabolic network for peanut based on RNA co-expression patterns of four embryo developmental stages (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">13</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM9">11b,c</a>), which may facilitate improvement of oil quality and content.</p><p>Peanut uses a unique <i>Rhizobium</i> infection mechanism for nitrogen fixation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Sprent, J. I. et al. Legume evolution: Where do nodules and mycorrhizas fit in? Plant Physiol. 144, 575â581 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2787">36</a></sup>, which may be more transferrable to nonlegume species than other mechanisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Charpentier, M. et al. How close are we to nitrogen-fixing cereals? Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 13, 556â564 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2791">37</a></sup>. We identified a total of 119 orthologous families of nodulation-related genes in 13 legume species (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">4.4</a>) with 81 (68.07%) conserved in all 13 (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM9">11d</a>). Peanut retained 95 families of 169 genes with 4 families missing and 40 experiencing more gene loss (29 families) or gain (11 families) than the sequenced diploids during evolution (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM9">11e</a>). These contained all genes required by other legume species for symbiotic nitrogen fixation. A phylogenetic tree of three ubiquitous symbiosis signaling pathway genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">14</a>) showed that genes in the <i>Arachis</i> species were distinct from, and ancestral to, those in other legumes, perhaps associated with the unique nodulation mechanism (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">4.4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">The origin and domestication of peanut</h3><p>Peanut resulted from a single hybridization between A and B genome species in South America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="FÃ¡vero, A. P., Simpson, C. E., Valls, F. M. J. &amp; Velo, N. A. Study of evolution of cultivated peanut through crossability studies among Arachis ipaensis, A. duranensis and A. hypogaea. Crop Sci. 46, 1546â1552 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2822">9</a></sup>, believed to be <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1151â1165 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2831">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Seijo, G. et al. Genomic relationships between the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea, Leguminosae) and its close relatives revealed by double GISH. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1963â1971 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2834">6</a></sup>. Comparison with the sequenced A and B genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2838">1</a></sup> supports <i>A. ipaensis</i> or a close relative as the peanut B subgenome progenitor with average &gt;99.5% identity, but much greater divergence (~97% identity) from <i>A. duranensis</i> (Supplementary Datasets <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM6">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig2">2c</a>). By characterizing <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values between all co-linear genes based on 8.21âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> <i>K</i><sub>s</sub>âyr<sup>â1</sup> (NeiâGojobori approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418â426 (1986)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2871">38</a></sup>, Bertioli et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2875">1</a></sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5a</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5.2</a>), we found that the divergence of diploid and tetraploid A or B genomes was dated to ~â0.42â0.47âMa and therefore was more ancient than previously thought, thus falsifying the possibility of human involvement in polyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2886">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Dillehay, T. D., Rossen, J., Andres, T. C. &amp; Williams, D. E. Preceramic adoption of peanut, squash, and cotton in northern Peru. Science 316, 1890â1893 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2889">39</a></sup>. The split of A and B subgenomes was estimated at ~â2.6âMa, as previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2893">1</a></sup>. Estimation of the splitting dates of 41 single-copy genes with BEAST2 again confirmed the earlier inference (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5.2.2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Evolutionary history of peanut."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Evolutionary history of peanut.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="830" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions of gene pairs in each species. Diploid A (Ad) and B (Bd) genomes diverged from one another about 2.6âMa, and from their corresponding subgenomes ~0.42â0.47âMa based on a mutation rate of 8.21âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> <i>K</i><sub>s</sub>âyr<sup>â1</sup> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418â426 (1986)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2922">38</a></sup>). <b>b</b>, Maximum likelihood tree of 52 varieties generated from 17.16 million SNPs. Color brackets indicate different groups. Topologies are supported by percentages of 1,000 bootstrap replicates indicated by branch values. Scale bar represents substitutions per site. <b>c</b>, Pattern of admixture analysis of the 52 accessions when <i>K</i>â=â3. Of the three major groups detected, accessions from A-genome, Pr-genome and one synthetic, ISATGR 184, clustered together as group 1 (red bars). All of the accessions belonging to the B-genome, K-genome, C-genome and E-genome clustered together with three synthetics (ISATGR 5, ISATGR 1212 and ISATGR 278-18) as group 2 (blue bars). The largest group was group 3 (green bars), consisting of all the tetraploids, except the synthetics. <b>d</b>, Evolutionary relationships and distribution of <i>Arachis</i> species, showing the hypothesized hybridization producing tetraploid <i>A. monticola</i> and the subsequent evolution of peanut into two subspecies and four (later six) varieties or ecotypes. Dashed line arrows AâD show the original <i>A. hypogaea</i> varieties were moved and domesticated independently to form var. <i>hypogaea</i> in Bolivia; Peruvian type (var. <i>hirsuta</i>) in Ancon, Peru; Valencia type (<i>fastigiata</i>) in Paraguay-central Brazil; and Spanish type (<i>vulgaris</i>) in the Guarani area (ParaguayâArgentinaâBrazil), respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1151â1165 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2961">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stalker, H. T. &amp; Wilson, R. F. (eds). Biology, speciation, and utilization of peanut species. in Peanuts Genetics, Processing, and Utilization Ch. 2 (AOCS Press, 2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2964">40</a></sup>. Accessions are shown based on collection site. <i>A.h</i>., <i>Arachis hypogaea</i>; ssp., subspecies; var., variant.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Among 81 species of nine sections in <i>Arachis</i>, peanut evolved from the biggest section, but its exact origin and domestication are still unclear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stalker, H. T. &amp; Wilson, R. F. (eds). Biology, speciation, and utilization of peanut species. in Peanuts Genetics, Processing, and Utilization Ch. 2 (AOCS Press, 2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d8005e2982">40</a></sup>. We resequenced 52 accession of 12 species including <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, as well as the wild tetraploid <i>A. monticola</i>, and 30 diverse peanut samples (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM10">12</a>). Phylogeny, admixture and principal component analysis (PCA) clustered the 52 accessions into three classes using SNP (legends of Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b,c</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">15a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">16a,b</a>). Fst (diversity paramteter) values between groups 1 and 3 (0.76094) or 2 and 3 (0.79683) suggested higher genetic distances and low genome exchange associated with ploidy difference (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis places <i>A. monticola</i> as basal after tetraploidization, with subsequent divergence of two subspecies and four (later considered six) varieties (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b</a>). The origin of <i>A. monticola</i> from <i>A. ipaensis</i> and some <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b,d</a>, earth map showing locations) was supported by similar levels of SNPs and InDels in <i>A. ipaensis</i>, <i>A. monticola</i> and many peanut varieties, but not in <i>A. duranensis</i> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM11">13</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">15</a>). Peanut was predicted to have been domesticated from <i>A. monticola</i> in northern Argentina<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst.
