Evol. 298, 1151â1165 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3052">5</a></sup>; however, our phylogenetic and sequencing data find <i>A. monticola</i> basal to subspecies <i>hypogaea</i> and <i>fastigiata</i> ecotypes. This indicated that peanut may have started from diverse subspecies <i>hypogaea</i> and been domesticated independently in different locations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stalker, H. T. &amp; Wilson, R. F. (eds). Biology, speciation, and utilization of peanut species. in Peanuts Genetics, Processing, and Utilization Ch. 2 (AOCS Press, 2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3069">40</a></sup>, for example, to the northwest evolving Peruvian ecotypes adaptable to drought (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5d</a>, arrow B) and to the southeast deriving Valencia and Spanish ecotypes independently (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b,d</a>, arrows C and D), which later spread worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stalker, H. T. &amp; Wilson, R. F. (eds). Biology, speciation, and utilization of peanut species. in Peanuts Genetics, Processing, and Utilization Ch. 2 (AOCS Press, 2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3079">40</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">14</a>). The phylogenetic tree classified most cultivated peanut accessions into two groups corresponding to the two subspecies, but showed clearly that subspecies <i>hypogaea</i> intermingled with modern vulgaris-type cultivars (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM10">12</a>) bred from inter-subspecies crosses, an approach used to breed widely adaptable, high-yielding cultivars in China.</p><p>The sequences of four synthetic tetraploids illustrate opportunities to diversify the peanut gene pool. Phylogenetic analysis grouped synthetics with diploids, indicating high genetic distance from natural tetraploids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b</a>). Synthetic tetraploids ISATGR 278 [<i>A. duranensis</i> (ICG 8138)âÃâ<i>A. batizocoi</i> (ICG 13160)] and ISATGR 5 [<i>A. magna</i> (ICG 8960)âÃâ<i>A. batizocoi</i> (ICG 8209)] seemed to undergo whole-genome duplication. Two other synthetics derived from reciprocal crosses contained 1.23- and 5.93-fold more genome content of <i>A. duranensis</i> than the B genome based on read mapping (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">11</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">17a</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5.3.2</a>). The A genome enrichment presumably resulted from non-random retention of parent chromosomes in offspring because of incompatibility, which further supports the emerging hypothesis that a species other than <i>A. duranensis</i>, which is more compatible with the B genome, is the A genome donor. A total of 17.16 million non-redundant SNPs and 4.52 million non-redundant InDels were identified from the 52 Arachis accessions (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM11">13c,d</a>). Synthetics contained higher numbers of SNPs and InDels than natural tetraploids (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM11">13a,b</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">15</a>), offering rich diversity in functional genes and neutral DNA markers.</p><p>A finding warranting further investigation is that <i>A. stenophylla</i> (EE) and <i>A. pintoi</i> (CC) showed diverse SNP patterns, mapped on both subgenomes with low read numbers and grouped between A and B genome accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b,c</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">17b</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">11</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM10">12</a>). We hypothesize that diploids with E or C genomes might have separately evolved into diploid A and B genomes, which, in turn, hybridized to form peanut (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig5">5b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Impact on peanut improvement</h3><p>The genome reveals candidate genes for many agronomically important peanut traits that have been genetically mapped (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">15</a>). Through BLAST analysis using flanking DNA markers, 40 quantitative traits such as seed size, yield and quality, resistance and plant characters were mapped to pseudomolecules (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">18</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM12">15</a>), revealing candidate genes. For example, red testa controlled by a single dominant gene was mapped to a region of 0.905 cM on chromosome 3 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig6">6a</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM13">16</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM14">17b</a>). Candidate genes WRKYs (including WRKY13 with cosegregated R202Q; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig6">6a</a>), MYB and bHLH family and cytochrome 450 genes relating to anthocyanidin biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lloyd, A. et al. Advances in the MYBâbHLHâWD repeat (MBW) pigment regulatory model: Addition of a WRKY factor and co-option of an anthocyanin MYB for betalain regulation. Plant Cell Physiol. 58, 1431â1441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3200">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kitada, C. et al. Differential expression of two cytochrome P450s involved in the biosynthesis of flavones and anthocyanins in chemo-varietal forms of Perilla frutescens. Plant Cell Physiol. 42, 1338â1344 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3203">42</a></sup> and anthocyanidin reductase and flavonoid 3â²-monooxygenase of the anthocyanidin biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lloyd, A. et al. Advances in the MYBâbHLHâWD repeat (MBW) pigment regulatory model: Addition of a WRKY factor and co-option of an anthocyanin MYB for betalain regulation. Plant Cell Physiol. 58, 1431â1441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3207">41</a></sup> were found in the locus (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.2.4</a>). Upregulation of anthocyanin synthesis genes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM14">17câe</a>; Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.2.3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.2.4</a>) may cause red seed color.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Candidate genes underlying seed size and color and foliar disease resistances."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Candidate genes underlying seed size and color and foliar disease resistances.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0402-2/MediaObjects/41588_2019_402_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="872" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Seeds with red and pink testa color, linkage mapping and the candidate-gene model with SNPs. Scale bars indicate 1âcm. <b>b</b>, Phenotypes of RILs with pod size segregation, BSA mapping by resequencing and QTL mapping of pod size (100 pod weight in RILs (Yueyou 92âÃâXinhuixiaoli)). Seed size QTLs were mapped on Chr07 (A07) and Chr12 (B02) using genetic mapping and QTL-seq approaches. Scale bar indicates 1âcm. <b>c</b>, Phenotypes of LLS-susceptible and LLS-resistant RILs from TAG 24âÃâGPBD 4. A Chr13 (B03) genomic region was mapped for both LLS and rust resistance. Scale bars indicate 5âcm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Functional analysis of candidate genes promises new information on the regulation of peanut seed size, an important yield component. Fine QTL mapping and bulk segregant analysis (BSA) using a recombinant inbred line (RIL) population, Yueyou 92 and Xinhuixiaoli (Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.1.5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.2.2</a>), identified the same candidate regions on Chr07 (0.87â1.95âMb in contigs 000199F) and Chr02 (4.41â5.91âMb in 000164F) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig6">6b</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM15">18a,b</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">20</a>), including 99 and 97 candidate genes, respectively. These 99 Chr07 genes included 19 candidates such as ABC transporters, oligopeptide transporter 5, histidine kinase 2, amino acid permease 3 and transcriptional regulator STERILE APETALA (SAP), which regulates seed development and seed size identity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pandey, M. K. et al. QTL-seq approach identified genomic regions and diagnostic markers for rust and late leaf spot resistance in groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 927â941 (2017)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3263">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kesavan, M., Song, J. T. &amp; Seo, H. S. Seed size: A priority trait in cereal crops. Physiol. Plantarum 147, 113â120 (2013)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3263_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Byzova, M. V. et al. Arabidopsis sterile apetala, a multifunctional gene regulating inflorescence, flower, and ovule development. Genes Dev. 13, 1002â1014 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3266">45</a></sup>. Histidine kinase 2 and SAP in <i>Arabidopsis</i> control shoot and seed growth and seed size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kesavan, M., Song, J. T. &amp; Seo, H. S. Seed size: A priority trait in cereal crops. Physiol. Plantarum 147, 113â120 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3273">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Byzova, M. V. et al. Arabidopsis sterile apetala, a multifunctional gene regulating inflorescence, flower, and ovule development. Genes Dev. 13, 1002â1014 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3276">45</a></sup>. Oligopeptide transporter 5, relating to embryo development and seed size, contained four missense SNPs between the two parents (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM15">18d</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.2.2</a>). Expression of the SAP and nearby F-box genes were upregulated substantially in the embryos or pods of the large seeded parent and RILs by RNA-seq (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM15">18c</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.1.7</a>). These 97 Chr12 genes on contig 000164 included an auxin transcription factor (ARF2) and three CYP78A6 playing key roles in seed size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Riefler, M., Novak, O., Strnad, M. &amp; Schmulling, T. Arabidopsis cytokinin receptor mutants reveal functions in shoot growth, leaf senescence, seed size, germination, root development, and cytokinin metabolism. Plant Cell 18, 40â54 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3293">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Fang, W. et al. Maternal control of seed size by EOD3/CYP78A6 in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant J. 70, 929â939 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3296">47</a></sup>, with CYP78A6 tandemly duplicated in the same region and ARF2 upregulated substantially in both the large seeded parent and RILs (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM15">18b,c</a>). One SNP and one InDel differentiate the promoter region of CYP78A6 between large and small seeded parents (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM15">18e</a>).</p><p>Resistance to two globally important foliar fungal diseases, leaf rust (caused by <i>Puccinia arachidis</i>) and late leaf spot (LLS) (<i>Cercosporidium personatum</i>), colocalizes to a common genomic region. A total of 1.73 billion high-quality reads of two parents (TAG 24 and GPBD 4, resistant and susceptible to both rust and LLS, respectively) and four resistant and susceptible pools from recombination inbred lines (RILs) revealed overlapping regions on Chr13 for rust (140.40â144.88 Mb) and LLS (140.80â144.71âMb; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">12</a>). This region harbored 216 (rust) and 171 (LLS) genes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM16">19b,c</a>), including TIR-NBS-LRR, pentatricopeptide repeat, glutathione <i>S</i>-transferase, serine/threonine kinase, and mitogen-activated protein kinase and calciumâdependent protein kinase pathway genes. An R-gene cluster with two conserved Tir-NBS-LRR genes includes one (AH13G54010.1) with resistance co-segregating SNPs between resistant and susceptible parents and bulks (G143854163A, G143855518A for rust; C143855539T, G143855898C for LLS), tracing to <i>A. cardenasii</i> via resistant variety GPBD 4, through ICGV 86855 (interspecific derivative). Because no missense SNP was closely related to resistance, AH13G54010.1 might be a candidate for resistance to both diseases. This genomic region seems to be translocated from Chr03 to Chr13 after tetraploidization (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig6">6c</a>) because QTL-seq analysis using the <i>A. duranensis</i> genome assembly identified rust and LLS resistance on Aradu.A03 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, N. &amp; Li, Y. Signaling pathways of seed size control in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 33, 23â32 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3334">48</a></sup>).</p><p>High oleic acid in seeds, contributing to better flavor and longer storage life of peanut products and benefitting human cardiovascular health, results from mutations in homeologous genes. We developed mutant lines with ~80% oleic acid from genetic backgrounds with only ~40% oleic acid (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">13</a>), and resequenced two Min6-A from EMS treatment of Minhua 6 and Min8-A from Î³-ray radiation of Minhua 8 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">13</a>; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM17">20</a>). Both mutants differed from wild type by mutations in homeologous microsomal oleoyl-PC desaturase genes, ahFAD2A (dysfunction mutation on AH09G33970 at 114,779,221 bp of Chr09, G673A/D225N for FAD2A) and ahFAD2B (frameshift on AH19G43590 at 154464257bp of Chr19), which confer high oleate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Jung, S. et al. The high oleate trait in the cultivated peanut [Arachis hypogaea L]. Isolation and characterization of two genes encoding microsomal oleoylâPC desaturases. Mol. Gen. Genet. 263, 796â805 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3350">49</a></sup> in Min8-B (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM17">20a,b</a>). The mutations were experimentally validated by both near-infrared spectrum and chemical analysis and Sanger sequencing of another mutant AOM7a513 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">21</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">13</a>). Locations of ahFAD2A and ahFAD2B on Chr09 and Chr19 of the tetraploid genome explain that both happened simultaneously, leading to high oleic peanuts.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>High oil and protein content and drought resilience (geocarpy) make peanut important for global food security. This high-quality genome assembly will accelerate breeding objectives, including improved yield and oil quality, and resilience to disease and abiotic stresses. Identification of mutations underlying large seeds and high oleate, as well as candidate genes or genomic regions for other important traits, provides insights into high-yield and quality formation and expedites breeding. The research community can now better capitalize on the value of peanut as a model for polyploid genome evolution and its contributions to improved yield, quality and resistance.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>A full description of the methods can be found in the Supplementary Information. No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The genome-associated experiments were not randomized, and the investigators were carefully allocated during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Sequencing</h4><p>DNA was extracted from leaf tissues of a single plant of <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Shitouqi (ssp. <i>A. h. fastigiata</i> var. <i>vulgaris</i>, the most widely cultivated peanut ecotype in the world) following a previously published protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Mayjonade, B. et al. Extraction of high-molecular-weight genomic DNA for long-read sequencing of single molecules. Biotechniques 61, 203â205 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3401">50</a></sup> and purified with Beckman Coulter Genomics AMPure XP magnetic beads. DNA quality was assessed by agarose gel electrophoresis and NanoDrop 2000c spectrophotometry, followed by Thermo Fisher Scientific Qubit fluorometry. A total of 204 single-molecule real-time cells were run on the PacBio RS II system, and 14 cells on the Sequel system, with P6/C4 chemistry (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.2</a>), thus producing 270.5-Gb subreads with a coverage of 100Ã of the peanut genome.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Assembly</h4><p>De novo assembly was developed on a large-scale Tanhe computer using the diploid assembly FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623â630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3420">51</a></sup>, including PacBio raw read correction, preassembly and contigs construction. The draft assembly contigs were followed by error correction using PacBio reads with the quiver algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3424">52</a></sup>. DNA was also sequenced using an Illumina HiSeq 2000 machine, and the quivered contigs were further polished by Illumina reads. Finally, potential contaminations were screened against National Center for Biotechnology Information bacteria, virus database and human genome to form the final contig assembly.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec13">Three-dimensional chromatin conformation capture sequencing</h4><p>To generate physical scaffolds for genome assembly, we generated Hi-C sequencing data by adapting published procedures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Belton, J. M. et al. Hi-C: a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods 58, 268â276 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3436">53</a></sup> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.4.1</a>). In brief, freshly harvested leaves were cut into 2- to 3-mm pieces and infiltrated in 2% formaldehyde, and crosslinking was stopped by adding glycine. The tissue was ground to powder and suspended in nuclei isolation buffer to obtain a nuclei suspension. Nuclei were digested with <i>Hind</i>III as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3446">18</a></sup>, marked by incubating with Klenow enzyme and biotin-14-dCTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289â293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3450">17</a></sup> generating blunt-end-repaired DNA strands, and ligated by T4 DNA polymerase. The extracted DNA was mechanically sheared to 200â300âbp sizes by ultrasound followed by size fractionation using AMPure XP beads. DNA fragments of 150â300âbp were blunt-end repaired and A-tailed, followed by purification through biotinâstreptavidin-mediated pulldown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3455">18</a></sup>. PCR amplification was performed after adapters were ligated to the Hi-C products. The PCR products were purified with AMPure XP beads, and the Hi-C libraries were quantified by quantitative PCR for Illumina HiSeq X-ten PE150 sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289â293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3459">17</a></sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">Scaffolding the PacBio assemblies with LACHESIS</h4><p>Hi-C unique paired-end sequence data were used to scaffold the PacBio assembly contigs using a software pipeline LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3471">24</a></sup>. The Hi-C sequences were aligned to the draft contig assemblies. The separations of Chicago read pairs mapped within draft contigs were clustered by agglomerative hierarchical clustering producing chromosomic groups. The contigs within the groups were constructed as trunks based on interaction strength among contigs, by selecting the most dependable trunks as roots for adding the rest contigs into suitable positions and producing a group with correct contigs order. Finally, the orientations of contigs within chromosomal groups were determined using weighted directed acyclic graph (WDAG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3475">18</a></sup> based on interaction strength between two contig directions.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">RIL population mapping and marker analysis</h4><p>Two RIL mapping populations of 978 F<sub>9</sub> lines and 343 F<sub>12</sub> lines were developed from the same crosses of Yueyou 92 (<i>A. hypogaea</i> var. <i>vulgaris</i>) and Xinhuixiaoli (<i>A. hypogaea</i> var. <i>fastigiata</i>) at different times by single-seed descent starting from F<sub>2</sub> generation. Specific locus-amplified fragment sequencing was performed with DNA from the parents and randomly chosen 314 RIL<sub>12</sub> lines and 267 RIL<sub>9</sub> lines using the specific locus-amplified fragment SNP calling method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Sun, X. et al. SLAF-seq: An efficient method of large-scale De novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS ONE 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3511">54</a></sup> (Biomarker Company), and sequencing reads were mapped to the reference genome (<a href="http://peanutbase.org/">http://peanutbase.org/</a>) using SOAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP: short oligonucleotide alignment program. Bioinformatics 24, 713â714 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3520">55</a></sup>. SNPs were called in the parents and in the RIL lines. Genotype calls were generated for every line of the two populations by summing up read counts. Markers were assigned to linkage groups by HighMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Liu, D. et al. Construction and analysis of high-density linkage map using high-throughput sequencing data. PLoS ONE 9, e98855 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3524">56</a></sup>. The order of the markers was determined using the maximum likelihood algorithm. Regression mapping in HighMap was used to determine the centimorgan distances per genetic linkage group.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Integrated linkage maps</h4><p>The two dense SNP maps described earlier were integrated with two previously published peanut linkage maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhou, X. et al. Construction of a SNP-based genetic linkage map in cultivated peanut based on large scale marker development using next-generation double-digest restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (ddRADseq). BMC Genom. 15, 351 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3536">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Peanut Marker Database (Kazusa DNA Research Institute, 2016); &#xA;http://marker.kazusa.or.jp/Peanut/&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3539">20</a></sup> (one 1,619-SNP linkage map and one refined integrated map containing 1,954 simple sequence repeat (SSR) or transposon markers after removing some contradictory markers) by ALLMAP software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: Robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3543">21</a></sup> based on assembling the contig sequences of the STQ genome. The maps of Yueyou 92âÃâXinhuixiaoli were set as the highest priority in the integration. The assumption is that at the 100-kb bin level recombination should essentially be zero. On this level, a regression of centimorgan position on both maps yielded <i>R</i><sup>2</sup> valuesâ&gt;â0.85 and often &gt;0.9, so the regression line could easily be used for interpolating the positions of the alternative map toward the corresponding position on the Yueyou 92âÃâXinhuixiaoli map. All Yueyou 92âÃâXinhuixiaoli markers went into the integrated map on their original position.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Constructing chromosome pseudomolecules</h4><p>The construction of pseudomolecules followed an automated procedure by the integration of the following datasets: (1) sequence assemblies of 7,232 contigs, (2) the high-density integrated linkage map with 14,619 markers as described earlier, and (3) Hi-C data with valid pair-end reads of 31,734,151 covering more than 99.6% of the total length of contigs sequences. Specifically, Hi-C data were used to map the contigs and clustered the contigs into scaffolds using the software LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3559">24</a></sup>. Then using the Hi-C alone assembled map and the integrated linkage maps, we assembled the whole chromosomes by ALLMAP software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: Robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3563">21</a></sup> with a priority of Hi-C assembled map versus integrated genetic map being 1:1. The Hi-C assembled chromosomal scaffolds were optimized for the arrangements and orientation of contig trunks in this step together with manual adjustment. Subsequently, the pseudomolecules were generated by concatenating the adjacent contig sequences with 100 âNâs, and were oriented and numbered in accordance with previously published maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3567">1</a></sup>. Finally, all contig sequences not anchored to chromosomes were constructed with 100 âNâs as linkers following the order of contig sizes.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">Validation of <i>A. hypogaea</i> genome assemblies with BACs</h4><p>To validate the genome assembly, we downloaded a total of 1,576 public BAC end sequence (BES) records of <i>A. hypogaea</i> in GenBank GSS database (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/FI498696.1">FI498696.1</a> to <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/FI503143.1">FI503143.1</a>) for analysis. These BESs were aligned to the reference genome through BLASTN with the criterion of &gt;95% aligning identity, &gt;90% aligned coverage for BESs, not located in pseudochromosome Chr00 and the insert size lower than 200âkb. The insertion lengths between a matched pair of BAC end sequences within the genome are about a 110-kb span on average (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">1.8</a>). We also performed all-to-all alignment (-minIdentityâ=â80â99, -minScoreâ=â100, --fastMap) of three available peanut BACs released in the GenBank<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ratnaparkhe, M. B. et al. Comparative analysis of peanut NBS-LRR gene clusters suggests evolutionary innovation among duplicated domains and erosion of gene microsynteny. New Phytol. 192, 164â178 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3597">22</a></sup> and the assemblies using BLAT.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec19">Annotation of genome and transcribed regions</h4><p>Gene models were predicted using EuGene 4.2 embedded in a fully automated pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinform. 3, 87â97 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3609">57</a></sup>. The annotation of the peanut genome assemblies was based on four datasets that included: (1) RNA-seq data (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM5">5a</a>); (2) reference protein predictions from <i>Arachis ipaensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3618">1</a></sup>, <i>Arachis duranensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438â446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3624">1</a></sup>, <i>Glycine max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3631">30</a></sup>, <i>Medicago truncatula</i> and <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>, as well as A<i>rabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis information resource (TAIR): Improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucl. Acids Res. 40, D1202âD1210 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3643">58</a></sup> from Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Samoluk, S. S. et al. First insight into divergence, representation and chromosome distribution of reverse transcriptase fragments from L1 retrotransposons in peanut and wild relative species. Genetica 143, 113â125 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3647">8</a></sup>; (3) previously released transcriptome 454 sequencing data (complementary DNA) sequences (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR1367372">SRR1367372</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR1368960">SRR1368960</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR1371390">SRR1371390</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR1377239">SRR1377239</a>); and (4) newly generated peanut PacBio Iso-Seq data. The RNA-seq datasets were derived from a total of 29 different tissues and conditions (Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2.1</a> and 2.2). The full-length transcriptome data were derived from the 29 evenly mixed previously described RNA samples and were generated by the Iso-Seq method (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2.3</a>) for supporting annotation.</p><p>AUGUSTUS, SNAP and GeneMark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637â644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3677">59</a></sup> were used for ab initio gene prediction, using model training based on coding sequences from <i>A. ipaensis</i>, <i>A. duranensis</i>, <i>G. max</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. RNA-seq and Iso-Seq reads were mapped onto the reference genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Cole, T. et al. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3694">60</a></sup> and Bowtie 2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Ben, L. et al. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3698">61</a></sup>), respectively. Hints with locations of potential intronâexon boundaries were generated from the alignment files with the software package BAM2hints in the MAKER package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3702">62</a></sup>. MAKER with AUGUSTUS was then used to predict genes in the repeat-masked reference genome. Genes were characterized for their putative function in the UniProt and KEGG databases. Completeness of gene spaces was evaluated with the BUSCO pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3706">24</a></sup>.</p><p>Genome-wide prediction of ncRNAs, such as rRNA, small nuclear RNA and miRNA, was performed in Rfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Kalvari, I. et al. Rfam 13.0: shifting to a genome-centric resource for non-coding RNA families. Nucl. Acids Res. 46, D335âD342 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3713">63</a></sup>. tRNA and rRNA were identified using tRNAscan-SE, and RNAmmer and miRNA were predicted using miRanda version 3.0 (<a href="http://www.microrna.org">http://www.microrna.org</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec20">Annotation of repeat region</h4><p>Conserved BLASTN search in Repbase and de novo prediction were performed to annotate repeat sequences. Repeat families were first de novo identified independently and classified using RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Smit, A. F. A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2015); &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3729">64</a></sup> (see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">2.4</a>). RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. Repeat Masker Open-4.0 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2015); &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3736">65</a></sup> was used to search and identify the repeats within the genomes. Repeats annotation in Repbase was also performed by RepeatMask, RepeatProteinMasker and TRF software and merged with de novo annotation.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec21">Gene differential expression analysis</h4><p>The normalized counts of gene expression were estimated using Cufflinks package based on the TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Cole, T. et al. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3749">60</a></sup> output results of the 29 samplesâ RNA-seq data analysis as described earlier. The fragments per kilobase per million mapped reads (FPKM) values of expression genes were calculated. The differential expression between homeologous genes was identified by FPKM if their fold change (FPKMa/FPKMb) was greater than 2 and the false discovery rate was â¤0.01.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec22">Syntenic analysis of peanut and its wild diploid genomes</h4><p>To identify chromosome structural changes between tetraploid peanut and two wild diploids, we analyzed subgenome synteny by plotting the positions of homeologous pairs of A- and B-subgenome within the context of the 20 chromosomes using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: An information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3761">66</a></sup>/MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and colinearity. Nucl. Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3765">67</a></sup> with at least five syntenic genes. Synteny of the A- and B-subgenomes versus diploid A and B genomes was compared, respectively, using the same software. To differentiate chromosome recombinations within the two subgenomes after tetraploidization, we also performed synteny of the two diploid genomes for comparison (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">Orthologous regions between peanut and the diploid species</h4><p>To determine the variations of chromosome insertions, deletions or substitutions, we identified orthologous regions in cultivated peanut and the two diploid species (<a href="https://peanutbase.org">https://peanutbase.org</a>) by BLASTN searches of the peanut genome using the Molecula of contigs from each diploid genome individually. The similarity between the peanut subgenomes and the diploid species <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> was presented in dot figures proportional to chromosome sizes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">3.3.4</a>). Segmental relationships along chromosomes were identified by reciprocal comparisons.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Genomic comparison of <i>A. hypogaea</i> with other legume species and <i>V. vinifera</i>
</h4><p>To investigate the origin and evolution of peanut genome, the evolutionary relationships of peanut, we compared its diploid ancestors and other genome-sequenced legume species including <i>G. max</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>M. truncatula</i>, as well as <i>V. vinifera</i>. We identified homologous proteins between <i>A. hypogaea</i> and five other legume genomes using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Moreno-Hagelsieb, G. &amp; Latimer, K. Choosing BLAST options for better detection of orthologs as reciprocal best hits. Bioinformatics 24, 319â324 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3827">68</a></sup> (E value 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>) and scanned syntenic blocks consisting of homologous genes among the 11 genomes including <i>V. vinifera</i> using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and colinearity. Nucl. Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3836">67</a></sup> with at least five syntenic genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">10</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">7</a>). To reconstruct the chromosomal evolution model of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, we inferred 16 legume basic chromosomes before LCT from <i>V. vinifera</i>, then constructed 16 legume common chromosomes (called Lu) after LCT from <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and then reconstructed the ancestral A and B genomes chromosome from Lu, which hybridized and evolved to 20 <i>A. hypogaea</i> chromosomes using the precise analysis of co-linear relationships.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Identification of orthologous genes</h4><p>Orthologous gene families among peanut, two wild species (<i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. duranensis</i>), and several other plant species including <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>G. max</i>, as well as <i>A. thaliana</i>, were identified using the OrthoMCL pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3889">69</a></sup>. The longest protein prediction from each gene was selected. Pairwise sequence similarities between all input protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Moreno-Hagelsieb, G. &amp; Latimer, K. Choosing BLAST options for better detection of orthologs as reciprocal best hits. Bioinformatics 24, 319â324 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3893">68</a></sup> with an E value cutoff of 10<sup>â5</sup>. Markov clustering of the resulting similarity matrix was used to define the ortholog cluster structure of the proteins. Comparative analysis of gene families and the copy numbers was performed among peanut and the other species for visualization with InteractiVenn using Custom Perl scripts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Heberle, H., Meirelles, G. V., Da Silva, F. R., Telles, G. P. &amp; Minghim, R. InteractiVenn: A web-based tool for the analysis of sets through Venn diagrams. BMC Bioinform. 16, 169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3899">70</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#Fig4">4</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">11</a>). Individual gene trees were then constructed using the maximum likelihood method using Mega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Jones, D. T., Taylor, W. R. &amp; Thornton, J. M. The rapid generation of mutation data matrices from protein sequences. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 8, 275â282 (1992)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3910">71</a></sup>. Changes of three important peanut gene families or pathways, such as fatty acyl metabolism, R gene and nitrogen symbiosis fixation, were analyzed in greater detail using BLAST searches, as well as GenomeThreader mappings to the peanut reference genome.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec26">Identification of nonredundant and duplicated genes in allotetraploid peanut genome</h4><p>Nonredundant genes in the cultivated peanut genome were identified based on the BLAST results of protein-coding genes between the two subgenomes. In brief, protein sequences extracted from A subgenome were aligned using BLAST against proteins from B subgenome, and vice versa. The best matches were retained and formatted to a two-column table of homeolog pairs. Duplicated genes were classified into two categories: (1) tandem duplicated genes if the multiple copies were consecutively located in the neighborhood, and (2) dispersed duplicated genes if not tandem.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec27">Acyl-lipid genes in cultivated peanut genome</h4><p>The protein sequences of all the annotated gene models from peanut and the two ancestors (<a href="https://peanutbase.org">https://peanutbase.org</a>) were aligned to two acyl-lipid gene datasets (885 <i>A. thaliana</i> and 829 soybean acyl-lipid genes; <a href="http://aralip.plantbiology.msu.edu/">http://aralip.plantbiology.msu.edu/</a>, <a href="https://www.soybase.org/">https://www.soybase.org/</a>) by BLASTP with an E valueâ&lt;â10<sup>â6</sup> and matching length â¥50%. Oil-related QTLs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Pandey, M. K. et al. Identification of QTLs associated with oil content and mapping FAD2 genes and their relative contribution to oil quality in peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.). BMC Genet. 15, 133 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3949">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shasidhar, Y. et al. Molecular mapping of oil content and fatty acids using dense genetic maps in groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). Front. Plant Sci. 8, 794 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3952">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Wang, M. L. et al. Genetic mapping of QTLs controlling fatty acids provided insights into the genetic control of fatty acid synthesis pathway in peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.). PLoS ONE 10, e0119454 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3955">72</a></sup> in peanut were also searched to find orthologs within those QTL regions. The identified acyl-lipid orthologs of three peanut species, also those from soybean, oil palm and rapeseed, were assigned to gene families by OrthoMCL (inflation value, 1.5) with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3959">69</a></sup>. The orthologous acyl-lipid genes in peanut were associated with RNA-seq expression data, and a weighted gene coexpression network analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: An R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinform. 9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3963">73</a></sup> was performed using R software (<a href="https://horvath.genetics.ucla.edu/html/CoexpressionNetwork/">https://horvath.genetics.ucla.edu/html/CoexpressionNetwork/</a>). Results were visualized by using Cytoscape software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Kohl, M., &amp; Wiese, S. &amp; Warscheid, B. Cytoscape: Software for visualization and analysis of biological networks. Methods Mol. Biol. 696, 291â303 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3971">74</a></sup>. To investigate enriched functions of identified coexpression modules, we used FatiGO to perform gene ontology enrichment analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Al-Shahrour, F. et al. FatiGO +: A functional profiling tool for genomic data. Integration of functional annotation, regulatory motifs and interaction data with microarray experiments. Nucl. Acids Res. 35, W91âW96 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3975">75</a></sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec28">NBS-LRR encoding genes</h4><p>NBS-encoding R genes in genomes of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipensis</i>, and also soybean, were screened using the Hidden Markov Model (HMMER3.0) and BLASTP. HMMER3.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Eddy, S. R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755â763 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d8005e3996">76</a></sup>) was used to search for the Pfam NBS (NB-ARC) family PF00931 domain (cutoff E valueâ&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>). BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4003">77</a></sup> was used to search TIR or no-TIR domain-containing NB-ARC R genes for class discrimination. Statistics were made in Excel to tell the changes and differentiation (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM8">10</a>). The classification and the evolution were predicted by phylogenetic analysis as following description. The localization of R genes was mapped among the reference genome using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: An information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4010">66</a></sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec29">Nitrogen symbiosis-related gene</h4><p>Nodulation-related genes were collected from two recent studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Peng, Z. et al. Transcriptome profiles reveal gene regulation of peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) nodulation. Sci. Rep. 7, 40066 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4022">78</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Qiao, Z., Pingault, L., Nourbakhsh-Rey, M. &amp; Libault, M. Comprehensive comparative genomic and transcriptomic analyses of the legume genes controlling the nodulation process. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 34 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4025">79</a></sup> in <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i>, and <i>G. max</i>. The protein sequences of nodulation-related genes were retrieved from 12 legume species (National Center for Biotechnology Information: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) and peanut. The orthologs were first determined by using BLASTP, BBH and OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4043">69</a></sup> (inflation value of 1.5 and other settings default).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec30">Resequencing</h4><p>Fifty-two accessions were chosen for DNA resequencing covering cultivated peanut, wild species and artificial tetraploids (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM10">12</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5.1.1</a>). All sequencing was performed with a HiSeq 2500 machine (Illumina), using 150-bp paired-end libraries. The raw reads from 52 accessions were filtered using trimmomatic v.0.36 and mapped to the reference genome using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589â595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4061">80</a></sup>. Variants were called using HaplotypeCaller and GenotypeGVCFs of Genome Analysis tool kit (GATK) v.3.8. The obtained SNPs were filtered using GATK filters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156â2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4065">81</a></sup> followed by HAPLOSWEEP v.1.0 to remove homeologous SNPs. The identified InDels were filtered using GATK filters. For phylogenetic analysis, the phylogenetic tree was constructed by SNPhylo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Lee, T. H., Guo, H., Wang, X., Kim, C. &amp; Paterson, A. H. SNPhylo: A pipeline to construct a phylogenetic tree from huge SNP data. BMC Genom. 15, 162 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4069">82</a></sup> (maximum likelihood method and 1,000 bootstraps) using the filtered SNPs. Admixture and PCA were performed for the 52 accessions (Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5.3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">5.4</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec31">Phylogenetic analysis of ARF</h4><p>To identify ARF homologs, we used the protein sequence from the <i>A. hypogaea</i> ARF gene as a BLAST query. Filtering for hits with an E valueâ&lt;â1âÃâe<sup>â5</sup>, identity of 50% with RNA-seq evidence resulted in the identification of 114 peanut proteins, with 28 and 28 proteins from AA subgenome and BB subgenome, respectively. For the construction of the phylogenetic tree, protein sequences from these 114 peanut ARF homologs were aligned using Clustal Omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Sievers, F. et al. Fast, scalable generation of high-quality protein multiple sequence alignments using Clustal Omega. Mol. Syst. Biol. 7, 539 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4093">83</a></sup> along with the above diploid gene models. Phylogenetic analysis was performed with MEGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725â2729 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4097">84</a></sup> (v.6.06). The final tree was estimated using the maximum likelihood method with a bootstrap value of 1,000 replicates.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec32">Phylogenetic analysis of R genes</h4><p>The alignment of NBS domains of R gene was performed with Clustal Omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Sievers, F. et al. Fast, scalable generation of high-quality protein multiple sequence alignments using Clustal Omega. Mol. Syst. Biol. 7, 539 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4109">83</a></sup>, using released sequences in NCBI (accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/FI498696.1/">FI498696.1</a> to <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/FI503143.1/">FI503143.1</a>). There are 661 R genes in the peanut reference genome. MEGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725â2729 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4121">84</a></sup> software (v.6.06) was used to perform phylogenetic analysis. The maximum likelihood method was used to infer the phylogeny based on the JonesâTaylorâThornton (JTT) matrix-based model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Jones, D. T., Taylor, W. R. &amp; Thornton, J. M. The rapid generation of mutation data matrices from protein sequences. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 8, 275â282 (1992)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4125">71</a></sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec33">Phylogenetic analysis of nodulation genes</h4><p>A phylogenetic tree was constructed by MEGA6 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725â2729 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4138">84</a></sup> using four nodulation genes found in all species for nodulation evolution and phylogenetic analysis. The best model was selected from Model Selection using the maximum likelihood method with 1,000 bootstrap replications.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec34">Integrating main QTLs to the reference genome</h4><p>Scores of previously published reports were searched for QTLs involving 40 traits covering peanut economically favorable traits and plant growth and development (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM12">15</a>). Their specific positions were identified by BLASTN alignments using the flanking markers of QTLs. A total of 136 main QTLs with ~8%â71% of phenotype variance explanation were mapped to the peanut genome assembly. A 4âMb sequence was considered at the flanks of the mapped markers to define the QTL coordinates and assess colocalization with candidate genes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM12">15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec35">Seed sizes and testa color gene analyses</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec36">QTL mapping</h4><p>A population was developed by crossing Yueyou 92 (pink testa, big seeds) and Xinhuixiaoli (red testa, small seeds) as mentioned earlier. Real hybrids were identified in F<sub>1</sub> plants with red testa, a dominant trait. First phenotyping of seed color was performed on 752 individual plants in the F<sub>2</sub> generation. Phenotyping of seed size and testa color were characterized in the RIL population of 267 lines containing 20 plants each, three replications for at least 2 years (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a>). The QTLs for seed size and testa color were mapped to the reference genome based on the genetic maps with 7,134 SNP markers derived from the population of 267 lines using the composite interval mapping (CIM) method of QTL IciMapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Meng, L., Li, H., Zhang, L. &amp; Wang, J. QTL Ici Mapping: Integrated software for genetic linkage map construction and quantitative trait locus mapping in biparental populations. Crop J. 3, 269â283 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4176">85</a></sup>. Candidate genes were searched by flanking DNA markers of QTLs (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec37">Candidate-gene evaluation</h4><p>Candidate genes for seed size and testa color within QTL regions were fine-mapped and evaluated based on QTL-seq in BSA and RNA-seq (Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.2.3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM1">6.1.7</a>), together with bioinformatics analysis. We mapped key genes by sequencing comparison with both randomly chosen RIL lines with big and small seeds, and natural varieties with big and small seeds. We also identified a key gene using RNA-seq analysis. Genes differentially expressed between RILs with big and small seeds or pink and red testa were selected.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec38">Bulk segregation analysis for seed size and foliar disease resistance</h3><p>The QTL-seq analysis was conducted from two RIL populations using the multiseason phenotyping data for three traits, namely, pod weight, rust resistance and LLS resistance. In brief, the bulks were made by pooling DNA from selected RILs with extreme phenotypes for these traits. For pod weight, the DNA from 54 RILs possessing low pod weight and 54 RILs with high pod weight were pooled from the population (Yueyou 92âÃâXinhuixiaoli). Similarly, DNA from 25 RILs each for resistance and susceptible RILs (TAG 24âÃâGPBD 4) were pooled to constitute four bulks, that is, resistant bulk for rust and LLS, and susceptible bulk for rust and LLS, respectively. The resistance parent GPBD 4 was an interspecific derivative of <i>A. cardenasii</i>, that is, the resistance source for both of the diseases. Together with four parents, a total of ten DNA samples were sequenced on Illumina HiSeq 2500. The sequencing data were analyzed using the QTL-seq pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Takagi, H. et al. QTL-seq: Rapid mapping of quantitative trait loci in rice by whole genome resequencing of DNA from two bulked populations. Plant J. 74, 174â183 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d8005e4209">86</a></sup> (<a href="http://genome-e.ibrc.or.jp/home/bioinformatics-team/mutmap">http://genome-e.ibrc.or.jp/home/bioinformatics-team/mutmap</a>) for calculating the SNP-index using the tetraploid genome assembly developed and reported in this article. The âSNP-index for each trait was then calculated by subtracting the SNP-index of one bulk from that of another bulk. The candidate-gene discovery was performed in the genomic regions with the highest âSNP-index.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec39">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Genome assemblies and resequencing data were available in BioProject of GenBank under accession numbers PRJNA480120 and SRR7617992, etc. (see Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0402-2#MOESM18">21</a>), respectively. The genome assemblies and annotations, transcriptome and PacBio Iso-Seq reads can also be accessed at <a href="http://peanutgr.fafu.edu.cn">http://peanutgr.fafu.edu.cn</a> and <a href="http://peanutgr.fafu.edu.cn/Download.php">http://peanutgr.fafu.edu.cn/Download.php</a>. All materials and other data in this study are available upon reasonable request.</p>
